High-Rise Multifamily and Nonresidential Building Costs
To better understand costs and other constraints related to multifamily and nonresidential 
building electrification, Rincon reviewed best-available existing resources on this topic, 
namely, the PCE 2035 Decarbonization Plan (2022), and The Rocky Mountain Institute The 
Economics of Electrifying Buildings: Medium Size Commercial Retrofits (2022)30. This 
document review is also supplemented by original contractor interviews conducted by 
Rincon for the upcoming Sacramento Electric Buildings Strategy (2022). Though not strictly 
City of San Mateo specific, the technical considerations of challenges to electrify 
oversized buildings are consistent between Sacramento and San Mateo, so are 
referenced here as recent region-specific resources for commercial electrification in 
Northern California.
Electrification Up-Front Costs and Other Considerations
While single family and low rise multifamily residential buildings tend to use similar 
equipment and operate in similar ways, commercial buildings and high-rise multifamily 
buildings tend to be highly variable. In original research conducted by Rincon for the City 
of Sacramento in the summer of 2022, all interviewees31 communicated that larger 
multifamily and nonresidential buildings are more complicated to electrify than single-
30 https://rmi.org/insight/economics-of-electrifying-buildings-medium-size-commercial-retrofits/
31 To better understand costs and other constraints related to multifamily and nonresidential building electrification, 
Rincon conducted interviews with three energy engineering consultants working on large building decarbonization in 
Sacramento. Five additional interviewees were identified through the City’s contact network to speak to this topic, 
including energy managers and directors of UC Davis Health. Sacramento Municipal Utility District, Brighton Energy, 
Association for Energy Affordability, TRC Companies.
87 of 332

--- page 88 ---
City of San Mateo
Building Inventory and Market Segmentation Memorandum (Task 1)
31
family homes and estimating costs is typically only possible on a project-by-project basis. 
This is because for most larger buildings, replacing a gas system with a similarly sized 
electric system is cost-prohibitive and therefore, often requires building re-engineering to 
determine appliance replacement and sizing options. For this reason, a detailed cost 
analysis for larger multifamily and commercial buildings was not developed and included 
here. Smaller buildings with domestic-sized space and water heating end uses, such as 
small to medium-sized retail or service buildings, have similar electrification costs as seen 
for single-family homes. 
However, some equipment types are similar across building types and the cost to electrify 
can be more readily analyzed at a high level. The PCE analysis notes that packaged 
commercial HVAC equipment, namely, packaged Single Zone (SZ) Gas Furnace with 
Cooling, are easy to electrify, as existing building 2-way A/C ordinances can cover most 
units without the requirement for incentives. The PCE Analysis also notes that the 
Packaged SZ Gas Furnace with Cooling offers a sizable opportunity to electrify, with 5,441 
Packaged SZ Gas Furnace with Cooling units across the County of San Mateo as shown 
below in Figure 17. Because of the relative cost parity and ease of adoption, there is 
already uptake of all-electric packaged SZ heat pumps, accounting for nearly 20% of 
analyzed existing commercial HVAC Equipment, as shown previously in Figure 15.
Figure 17 PCE 2035 Decarbonization Plan Commercial HVAC Equipment Analysis
PCE analyzed commercial rooftop packaged unit costs, finding that rooftop heat pumps 
are nearly identical to gas-fired rooftop packaged units, even without additional 
incentives, as shown below in Figure 18. This indicates that local policy and potential new 
incentives to retrofit using heat pump RTUs could be an impactful and cost effective 
strategy to achieve widespread commercial building electrification. 
88 of 332

--- page 89 ---
City of San Mateo
Building Inventory and Market Segmentation Memorandum (Task 1)
32
Figure 18 PCE 2035 Decarbonization Plan Commercial Rooftop Package Unit Cost 
Analysis
The Rocky Mountain Institute (RMI) ‘The Economics of Electrifying Buildings: Medium-Size 
Commercial Retrofits’ Report notes that local policy, or expectations of new local 
building electrification policies, will be a key driver in advancing commercial energy 
electrification, as portfolio owners will want to avoid stranded assets unless they 
undertake electrification. The RMI report focused on the electrification of HVAC systems 
from rooftop units (RTUS) with Heat Pump Rooftop Package Units (RTUs), on a 
prototypical 50,000 sq ft office building. 
The RMI report modeled 5 cases to assess the economics of RTU retrofits: Case 1: No 
electrification, Case 2: Partial electrification, Case 3: Full electrification, Case 4: efficient 
electrification, Case 5: efficient electrification + demand management, Case 6: 
Efficient electrification + demand management and PV. These cases, found that in the 
cases of more temperate climates like Las Vegas and Seattle, building owners could 
89 of 332

--- page 90 ---
City of San Mateo
Building Inventory and Market Segmentation Memorandum (Task 1)
33
achieve either neutral or positive 20-year net present value through full electrification 
(Seattle), or with full electrification with demand management and solar photovoltaics 
(Las Vegas), as shown in Figure 19.
Figure 19  RMI Report Exhibit 3: The 20-year Net Present Value of Each Case by City32
The results from the RMI study have three important San-Mateo-relevant takeaways, 
which is that 1) electrification policy, with electrification-specific prescriptive language 
for commercial building owners will be critical in actually electrifying commercial 
buildings, and 2) electrification of commercial building electric retrofits will likely have to 
be paired with a suite of demand management, energy efficiency, or renewable energy 
projects in order to make building electrification economical for building owners, and 3) 
heat pump rooftop package units are the most promising electrification solution for 
commercial building electrification, offering a replicable model for a 1:1 equipment 
swap-out. 
Case Studies
In addition to the PCE analysis, Rincon identified all-electric retrofit case study projects 
related to offices, schools, and restaurants. Schools (primary and secondary), restaurants 
(full-service and quick-service), and hospitals represent large commercial gas users in the 
City of San Mateo. Case studies were pulled from The Redwood Energy Pocket Guide to 
All-Electric Retrofits,33 the William J Worthen (WJW) Foundation Building Decarbonization

Office
IMMIX LAW OFFICE, PORTLAND, OR (11,615 SQUARE FEET; ALL-ELECTRIC RETROFIT)
The building utilizes a highly efficient dedicated outside air system (DOAS) with heat 
recovery ventilation (HRV) for their HVAC system, which separates heating and cooling 
from the ventilation system to allow for optimal control of each of these critical building 
functions. The new HVAC system is comprised of a 16-ton Mitsubishi variable refrigerant 
flow (VRF) and four Ventacity VS1000RT HRVs. The HVAC system heats and cools 30 office 
spaces, five conference rooms, a lunchroom, exercise room, two restrooms, and open 
common spaces. The project resulted in increased occupant comfort, improved indoor 
air quality, lower energy bills (between $300 to $700 per month to heat, cool, and 
ventilate the building), saved roof space, and precise temperature and humidity control. 
The building has seen a 63% reduction in energy usage.36
100 AVENUE OF THE AMERICAS, MANHATTAN, NY (>20,000 SQUARE FEET; ALL-ELECTRIC RETROFIT)
This building was retrofitted to meet all winter space heating needed for exterior offices 
with waste heat from the air conditioning in the interior core of the building during the 
winter through an all-electric multizone heat pump HVAC system. Multizone systems are 
best for larger (20,000 square feet or more), more diverse buildings. While only 6% of HVAC 
systems in California are multizone systems that could be replaced with multizone heat 
pump technology, these buildings represent 37% to 75% of the GHG emissions from all 
buildings. They are technically complex to design and require highly skilled installers but 
also represent the largest GHG reduction opportunities.37
SONOMA CLEAN POWER HEADQUARTERS, SANTA ROSA, CA (15,000 SQUARE FEET; ALL-ELECTRIC RETROFIT)
The building was originally built in 1979 in Downtown Santa Rosa and retrofitted with all 
new insulation, all-new windows, all-new siding, and all-new mechanical systems. As part 
of the retrofit, the building installed Daikin rooftop units with variable air volume delivery 
and thermofusers at the zone level. The project also included its own microgrid and 
electric vehicle charging, which required an electrical service upgrade.38

School
CALIFORNIA STATE UNIVERSITY, CALIFORNIA
The CSU Chancellor’s Office released a building decarbonization framework for the CSU 
system, which includes tools to evaluate central plan equipment, thermal overlap 
calculations for each campus, and a life cycle cost calculation tool for different 
decarbonization strategies. The framework provides all 23 campuses with a technical 
roadmap for replacing fossil fuel infrastructure with clean, electrified alternatives. The 
associated heat recovery potential and natural gas reduction at each campus was 
analyzed. The Sacramento CSU campus currently uses 78,164 million British Thermal Units 
per hour (MBTU) for cooling and 52,739 MBTU for heating. The heat recovery potential 
estimated for the building after adding thermal energy storage on site is 64% and the 
estimated natural gas reduction potential is 422,000 therms.39
CHATHAM UNIVERSITY EDEN HALL CAMPUS, PITTSBURGH, PA (ALL-ELECTRIC NEW CONSTRUCTION)
The campus is the world’s first fully self-sustained and zero net energy (ZNE) university 
campus. The campus includes 46 geothermal wells, an on-campus water treatment site, 
and a 40-acre farm. The campus kitchen was designed based on extensive energy 
analysis focusing on commercial kitchen equipment and HVAC systems. The analysis 
showed that induction equipment would reduce total kitchen energy consumption by 
over 50% compared to a traditional gas kitchen. The campus also installed demand-
based exhaust hoods with integrated heat recovery, a geo-exchange heat pump, and 
radiant heating and cooling systems for a low-energy HVAC system. The facility operates 
with an energy usage of approximately 60% below the typical full-service restaurant.40 
Restaurant
OYSTERMAN SEAFOOD BAR & KITCHEN, LONDON, ENGLAND; RETROFIT (INDUCTION UNITS)
The restaurant switched from gas to induction units, which allowed the restaurant to serve 
guests at a significantly faster rate. Gas ranges and fryers are half as efficient at 
transferring heat as electric ranges and fryers (35% vs. 75%), and electric ranges boil water 
twice as fast as gas ranges. Electric kitchens, therefore, use half or less as much heat to 
cook food and require half as much air conditioning. Electric equipment also takes less 
space to do the same amount of cooking, making it easier for a kitchen to expand its 
cooking capacity. In addition to these benefits, Oysterman also experienced fewer chef 
burns with their induction burners and found cleaning to be much easier, ultimately 
reducing staff turnover.41
BENIHANA JAPANESE STEAK HOUSE, VARIOUS LOCATIONS, U.S.; RETROFIT (INDUCTION GRILL)
The first Benihana steakhouse was started in New York City in 1964 and has since grown 
to 116 restaurants. Benihana performs teppanyaki cooking on steel grills. Recently the 
39 CalState Office of the Chancellor. 2022. CSU Decarbonization Framework.
40 William J Worthen Foundation. March 2022. The Building Decarbonization Practice Guide Volume 5: All-Electric 
Residential + Commercial Kitchens.

restaurant chain converted its gas teppan grills to electric resistance and induction grills, 
which can cook meats faster, hotter, and more efficiently than gas while reducing the 
risk of uncontrolled fires.42
HOTEL MARCEL, NEW HAVEN, CT; NEW ALL-ELECTRIC CONSTRUCTION
The hotel has an all-electric commercial kitchen, which uses induction, including an 
induction wok setup.43 Induction woks are not common in American commercial 
kitchens, however, they are becoming increasingly popular in Mainland China, Taiwan, 
Hong Kong, and Macau. Many commercial options exist on the market for induction 
cookers that can accommodate concave pans (such as woks). 15 million induction 
cookstoves were sold in Mainland China in 2020, with a growth of nearly 4% from the 
previous year.44 
MICROSOFT REDMOND CAMPUS CAFETERIA, REDMOND, WA; NEW ALL-ELECTRIC CONSTRUCTION
The Microsoft Redmond Campus serves over 12,000 meals a day in 77,000 square feet of 
all-electric kitchens. The project had to overcome barriers such as equipment availability, 
throughput considerations, and station design. Microsoft has noted a positive response 
from the industry in the past few years enabling the transition for them.45
High-Rise Multifamily
BAYVIEW TOWER, SEATTLE, WA
The 100 unit high rise multifamily building was retrofit to be all electric using the Origin 
heat pump water heater system that provides all needed water heating components 
(heat pump, storage tank, valves, controls, etc..) on one skid to maximize ease of 
installation. The system is expected to reduce energy use by 59%. 46
ELIZABETH JAMES HOUSE, SEATTLE, WA
Elizabeth James House is a four story, 60 unit low income senior apartment building built 
in 1968. This project had a central plan of four ECO2 heat pumps, with three existing 120 
gallon tanks in a series swing configuration. In addition, the system kept the three electric 
water heaters, and a 175 gallon swing tank was installed.29
Recommendations from Interviewees

asked contractors what recommendations they have for the City or utility provider for 
reducing barriers to electrification in the City. While some of these recommendations may 
be specific to the City of Sacramento, many of these recommendations hold for the City

of San Mateo, to account for the viewpoint of contractors and building developers 
dealing with complex commercial building stock. These recommendations included:
•
Alleviate the burden on the customer for upgrading older, last-mile equipment 
such as transformers and services. Having to upgrade this equipment can kill a 
project. SMUD (in San Mateo, PG&E) could identify transformers that are 
already at peak capacity and deal with them pro-actively, or identify grant 
funds to help cover these costs.
•
More workforce training and work with the unions is needed to prepare the 
workforce for electrification work.
•
Encourage projects to build-in more hot water storage to help avoid an 
increased peak electrical load.
•
Develop policy to avoid over-estimation of panel needs, which can trigger 
larger-scale electrical upgrades that are prohibitively expensive. Default 
calculations on equipment tend to overestimate peak load and using real 
data from a building instead can help avoid ballooning expenses.
•
Adjusted rate structures could be an incentive for early electrification 
adopters, since this would help guarantee on-bill savings in many cases. Peak 
demand for commercial customers currently sets their billing rate. Many 
customers are on the edge of hitting their peak demand charge and 
electrification would bump them into a higher tier, creating a disincentive to 
electrify. A specific commercial electrification rate with a discount would help 
remove this barrier. This suggestion item would require coordination with PCE. 
•
Expedited permits could create an incentive for commercial customers to 
electrify, especially on smaller jobs. Ideally, the permit could be filled out online 
over-the-counter with a simple set of questions with a permit turn-around rate 
of 24 hours.
•
A building performance standards approach could work well for commercial 
buildings. San Francisco’s program is recommended as a model. San 
Francisco’s program is self-reporting but is validated. Buildings over 10,000 
square feet are required to complete a level 1 audit; buildings over 50,000 
square feet are required to complete a level 2 audit, and reporting 
requirement is every five years. Setting up the reporting system online with 
simple report requirements and very low rigor at the start would help get the 
most buildings into the system.
•
Releasing a building performance standards program under something other 
than an electrification strategy may be the best tactic. This approach could 
appear more feasible, and avoid misunderstandings on potential for 
mandatory upgrades.
Current Incentives
Current electrification programs and rebates are available for commercial and multi-
family buildings in the City of San Mateo from PCE, TECH, and BayREN are summarized in 
Table 88. All residential appliance incentives described in Table 66 should also apply to

From: Gerard Mennino i
Sent: Wednesday, August 9, ‘40 PM

To: Sustainability & Infrastructure Commission
Subject: Comments on Rincon electrification report

DearS & |commission members,

Thanks for all you do for San Mateo. Ahead of your review of the Rincon Consulatants draft report on building
electrification, and based on the draft report, | have a few observations to share.

1. Above all is, this is a critical issue - global warming is clearly the worst threat that humanity has faced in millenia, but
also, San Mateo city has emerged as a leader in municipal climate change action, and this report and its implementation
has enormous potential to make a big difference throughout California and beyond. | encourage you to put every effort
into making this process a success - it may be one of the most important contributions to the planet that you make in
your career, and will definitely be more history-changing than almost anything that almost all the rest of us do!

2. I’m very happy to see a clear statement of the importance of equity. To eventually stop burning methane, everyone
will need to be on board, so both from a fairness and effectiveness point of view, we need to create solutions that can
work for everyone. As much as the effectiveness criteria need to be in line with equity, | hope that the equity criteria will
also be in line with effectiveness. | have seen programs in other jurisdictions that carried out very elaborate and very
expensive whole-home retrofits on a small number of houses, in a way that was more political than effective, so | hope
that any San Mateo programs manage to maximize both effectiveness and equity.

3. The world has a very limited budget of further emissions that can be released. | urge you to focus on maximizing tons
of CO2 avoided, over ‘complete’ solutions. For instance, since water heating accounts for half of all domestic gas usage,
water heaters are replaced more than furnaces and cooktops, and the economics of water heater electrification are the
most compelling, it would make sense to push immediately (i.e. in months, not years) to increase the rate of water
heater electrification on burnout, which is currently estimated at only 1-3%. Every gas water heater that is replaced this
year will still be polluting in 15 years time, so moving fast on the lowest hanging fruit is important. It is important to have
a comprehensive plan to electrify everything, but not everything can be done at once.

4. A second key issue with domestic water heating is that the BAAQMD will meet in late 2024 to determine if their
proposed regulations on water heaters should go into effect in 2027. When these regulations were proposed, many
board members expressed concerns about their feasibility - equity, funding, supply chain, vendor education etc. If the
regulations go ahead, it will be a colossal achievement - turning off half the domestic gas emissions from an area of 7
million people. If San Mateo city can move quickly on encouraging voluntary electrification of water heaters at scale, it
will be an important proof that the rest of the Bay Area can implement these changes. Again, it will allow San Mateo
innovation to have a huge effect, far beyond its borders.

Thank you for all the work you do, and | wish you every success in greening San Mateo.

-Gerard Manning.

From: Val Armento

Sent: Friday, September 8, 2023 5:29 PM
To: Sustainability & Infrastructure Commission
Subject: Sustainable Building Strategy — Project Update; SIC (great acronym) Agenda Item #3,

Sept. 13, 2023

I am adamantly opposed to the idea of targeting January 1, 2025 as a date for requiring
electric appliances upon appliance replacement in single family homes.

The report states “Building energy use represents 38% of communitywide greenhouse
gas emissions in San Mateo” which means 62% of GHG emissions come from elsewhere,
Focus there.

The report notes “At the regional level, in May 2023, the Bay Area Air Quality
Management District (BAAQMD) adopted zero nitrogen oxides (NOx) standards that will
prohibit the sale and installation of gas water heaters starting in 2027 and gas furnaces
in 2029 in the nine-county Bay Area. These standards are the most ambitious in the
country and outpace the state’s goal of banning the sale of new gas furnaces and water
heaters throughout the state starting in 2030.” There is no need for the City of San
Mateo to have an even more ambitious schedule. Are you trying to lay the groundwork
for litigation?

The Building Inventory and Market Segmentation Memorandum (Task 1) Building for the
Future — City of San Mateo Sustainable Buildings Strategy (Version 2) notes that
building vintage is a key factor in determining hurdles to electrification and that 70% of
the homes in the City of San Mateo were built before 1970 and “could potentially need
panel upgrades or other electrical work prior to full electrification.” It also notes
countywide the “vast majority of water heaters run on natural gas” especially in single
family homes. Further, space heating in “single family owned and rented buildings still
use gas most commonly.” When it comes to appliances, 60% of all stoves are gas
fueled.

I live in a home described in the preceding paragraph and it would require an electrical
panel upgrade because the fuse box is below 100 amp. Buried in footnote 22 on page 20
of the report (42 out of 70 in the packet) is the comment “The amp cutoff for electric
panel upgrade is widely understood to be 100 amps or higher- a building with below 100
amps likely will need a panel upgrade to accommodate all-electric appliances.” The
report itself states: “Assuming that home electrification does require an electric panel
upgrade (particularly for homes with electric panels less than 100 amps): * $28,003-
$31,948” Those are dollar figures as of the compilation of the report.

Furthermore, the report does not discuss the likely domino effect of a panel

upgrade. Will PG&E/PCE have additional exterior requirements? Do walls need to be
opened, thereby requiring sheetrock and painting? Will counters need to be

109 of 332


--- page 110 ---
reconfigured? What hidden costs and complexities have not been disclosed by all the
gung-ho proponents, presuming they have even thought of or considered them?

Several pages in the report attempt to explain away the financial hit by pointing to
incentives, none of which are guaranteed and all of which are after-the-fact rebates or
tax credits, assuming one qualifies. Not realistic; no thanks.

No where in the report of the Analysis Framework Memo incorporating Equity and
Effectiveness Electrify San Mateo | Building for the Future: City of San Mateo
Sustainable Buildings Strategy is there any discussion about the fact the state does not
currently have (nor will it have for several decades) enough reliable non-fossil fuel
energy for all the anticipated electrification. Similarly, there is no discussion or even
acknowledgement that there will not be enough reputable contractors to perform the
necessary work in the proposed truncated timeframe. The impact on the elderly and
middle class are ignored. Also lacking is any discussion of the disruption/inconvenience
to residents while such work is going on. Undoubtedly these items are not mentioned
because they require airing negatives.

While I support efforts to improve the environment, I also believe those in existing
single-family homes are entitled to freedom of choice in their personal spaces. Peninsula
Clean Energy “is committed to delivering 100% renewable and carbon free electricity by
2025.” Let’s see if that actually happens. The City Council already has proposed 2030
as the target date for termination of gas fuel infrastructure, which the consultants are
supposed to evaluate. Let them do that as objectively as they can. The state has an
“ambitious climate goal of carbon neutrality by 2045.” Enough already.

City of San Mateo residents do not need a mandated date for electrifying their
appliances.

Valerie Armento

From: Wendy Chou

Sent: Saturday, September 9, 2023 6:58 PM
To: Sustainability & Infrastructure Commission
Subject: Decarbonization matters to San Mateo

Dear Sustainability & Infrastructure Commissioners,

| am grateful to the Commission for its leadership and foresight in implementing the decarbonization objective for the
City of San Mateo and for releasing the draft study for review. San Mateo is one of just a handful of U.S. cities taking
decarbonization as seriously as the issue deserves. Kudos to you. At this time, however, | would also like to urge you to
continue the forward momentum on this process. We need to keep supporting decarbonization —through all means
possible, but specifically, by focusing on building electrification.

It is worth it to eliminate fossil fuel appliances from our city structures for the following reasons:

1) The warming climate, which is directly related to emissions of natural gas/methane, will have damaging effects on the
lives and livelihoods of San Mateans. We have seen that impacts include everything from extreme heat (which is
dangerous to children, the elderly, and the significant number of residents who lack air conditioning), fiercerand wetter
storms, and more frequent and more destructive wildfires. These are not crises that San Mateans want, nor deserve.
These are crises that interact and overlap since climate change is a threat multiplier.

2) The methane gas we burn comes from dangerous practices such as hydraulic fracturing that pollute the water and
air and imperil frontline communities.

3) Burning methane in our homes here in San Mateo is an unsafe practice with many identified health risks, for instance,
from gas stoves.

4) Switching to electric will reduce costs in the long run (and far sooner when including incentives). Rewiring America's
electrification guide estimates "Households will save on average $1,800 a year by going electric". As technologies begin
to scale, the costs will drop further. NREL reported in a recent model / study that "Higher levels of electrification with
rapid advancements in the cost and performance of end-use electric technologies can result in net energy system

savings—up to $800 billion in the high electrification scenario."

5) Importantly, here in the Bay Area we are fortunate to have certain municipalities and CCAs in the vanguard of
electrification. For example, a wide variety of existing Peninsula Clean Energy rebates, newer tax credits created by the
Inflation Reduction Act, and other available funding makes electrification more affordable (as the Rincon report
indicates: “Whole home electrification could be less expensive for building owners in San Mateo than replacing
appliances with gas, once rebates are included"),

Those who oppose decarbonization and want to choose business as usual are merely in denial that the future is already
getting hotter, more expensive, less predictable, and more deadly. | choose not to let that scenario play out.
Decarbonization is the better choice both from a moral and financial standpoint. With so many common-sense reasons
to accelerate electrification in San Mateo, | am hopeful that you will continue to facilitate our progress on this issue.

Thank you for your dedicated service and commitment to the public welfare.

From: Dave Santos

Sent: Sunday, September 10, 2023 2:52 PM
To: Sustainability & Infrastructure Commission
Subject: Converting your Home or Building to Electric - Costs and Implementation

San Mateo's Sustainability & Infrastructure Commission:

| am writing to address my concerns that the commission is fast tracking converting
homes from gas to electric appliances.

Gas appliances provide affordable, reliable energy. Total electrification is neither
economical nor logical. It removes an alternative energy supply and replaces it with a
single unreliable energy source. There will be no backup during electrical outages
which will increase as demand (including new construction) on the grid increases
exponentially. PCE, the local energy supplier, would benefit due to their monopoly
status . PCE relies on wind and solar generation, and both are unpredictable (meaning
more outages). Standby power currently is provided by PG & E through an energy
banking system which is fossil fuel in nature.

Interrupting the current energy supply will be costly. There will be formidable logistics
that need to be considered. This plan will require major upgrades by PG & E's
infrastructure, massive engineering and redesigning substations, distribution networks,
capacity increases and scheduling new electrical connections. PG&E will have to
severe or abandon their natural gas connections to all buildings. Do you feel
comfortable given PG&E's safety record that this will happen safely and smoothly and
be ready in a less than a year and a half?

| support your efforts but feel that it is myopic to do the necessary due diligence to
implement this in 2025.

Unlike the Community Flood and Storm Protection Initiative, information on this has not
been disseminated to homeowners.

Questions that come to mind are:

What is the city going to do to ramp up their infrastructure to accomplish this?

How will this be implemented? What information is going to be shared with the
homeowners and when?

What is the conversion going to cost a homeowner? Are there additional costs involved
if this is an older home?

118 of 332


--- page 119 ---
Who will be paying for all these costs?

Can PG&E supply enough energy to handle the increased demand?

Will there be enough licensed electrical contractors to meet the demand?
How will PG&E handle a tidal wave of new requests?

Will PG&E have the financial resources to upgrade their distribution system, install new
wiring and transfer stations?

Does anyone remember Stanford's three-day outage last year because of their
dismantling of their gas supplies cogeneration plant? Who will be accountable during
this conversion?

The reality of the situation for most people is the cost. | am looking for specific city
guidance regarding a cost breakdown and procedures. This is not a concern for
renters, but for home and building owners this is important.

It is my understanding that this initiative to end gas for existing homes/buildings goes
beyond codes set by the state; | am concerned that the commission fails to
comprehend and quantify the enormous possible home and business owner expense.

This is not simply a matter of electrical replacements, it’s also the upgrade and/or
addition of electric circuit panels, finding space to install such panels, increasing
electric service with PG&E (dealing with overhead/underground wiring replacements),
rewiring a homes and buildings for circuit placement and more. For example, for a new
heating system, depending on the nature of what needs to be replaced, an exterior
heat pump could be necessary. Researching this, it is an involved and complicated
project. A homeowner(depending on their house size) may end up needing several
exterior heat pumps or must wait hours for heat and hot water.

The uncharted territory of the proposed changes has not been well thought out at this
time. Most homes have gas furnaces and hot water heaters. Should your heater go
out, necessitating an all-electric replacement, doing so would mean no gas to the
existing appliances for up to 3 weeks. Even a stove replacement could end up with
ancillary costs for kitchens to accommodate the new electric appliances. Adding
electrical panels could also mean opening walls, replacing sheet rock, painting, and
cosmetic touchups.

Much like the homeowner insurance crisis, this would be perfect storm of an escalating
burden of costs that may adversely affect older residents, middle income families and
young families struggling to meet mortgage, property taxes, homeowner, and
earthquake insurance expenses.

119 of 332


--- page 120 ---
The city is asking for support to upgrade their storm system, do you think homeowners
will be receptive to voting for this proposal with this new proposed change for
electrification that has no price tag?

| am concerned about the effects of global warming and climate change. | urge the
commission to explore other options that are viable alternatives to reduce global
warming and the city’s carbon footprint. It’s important to explore the best courses of
action to reduce our carbon footprint in a more cost-effective way.

To reiterate, this plan will drastically impact on our energy supply, reliability, utility
providers, and pocketbooks.

The commission has good intentions . However, the home and building owners of San
Mateo deserve better than a hastily rushed and ill-conceived plan. Adopting such a
plan would have a drastic impact on the city's energy supply, reliability, utilities and
owner's finances. There needs to be more due process before impractical and costly
regulations are approved.

Thank you for your consideration in this matter.

Sincerely,

Dave Santos

--- page 122 ---
Dave Clark
San Mateo Heat Pump Advocates

Friday September 8th 2023

| head up Heat Pump Advocates, a group which has 120 members that supports G2G (garage
to garage) electrification with other San Mateo residents. Many thanks to the city and our SIC
commissioners for moving ahead with this vital initiative. This work will be built on by other
cities and increase the speed that our communities can react and create viable policies. This
is a complicated issue, but we now have a clear direction with steps. | will advocate for a
continuation of this work. This document is labelled Task 1, and some remarks may fall out of
scope if there are additional modules.

Previous comments from residents reinforce the perils of climate change and the need to act
swiftly. Having more of an engineering / business mindset, our comments are of a technical
nature.

KEY CHALLENGES FOR GAS TO ELECTRIC UPDATES

e Economics - The consumer cost aspect is addressed, what will the outlay be for the city,
county?

e Workforce - State-wide shortage of skilled labor. Can the city tie into green workforce
initiatives? Would pre-wiring expedite electrification projects.

e Grid Capacity - Recent papers confirm that grid will not be adequate in the next 15 years
for full residential electrification (EV / appliances) unless methods such as DER are
deployed.
https://xenetwork. org/ets/episodes/episode-205-rebuilding-the-grid-from-the-bottom-up/

See also recent CPUC activity on Distributed Renewable Energy resources. How do we

get a head start on managing behind the meter resources, energy equity, community
based designs, and community-based resilience?

BIGGEST BANG FOR BUCK

This approach is referenced as "complete solutions" in another letter. Why target residential
stoves when they account for only 2% of emmissions (LBL) and require a 40A /240 service?
As this LBL study suggests:

https://homes |bl.gov/publications/challenges-and-opportunities-hame

The least cost approach to at least halve carbon emissions and energy use is to replace gas furnaces, boilers, and
water heaters with heat pumps, add onsite PV, and do basic air sealing and insulation. Replacing gas clothes
dryers and cooking is a lower priority because they consume a lot less energy and emit a lot less carbon; these
items can be re-prioritized for decarbonization at the time of appliance replacement — thus reducing the hard
upfront costs. Staged, partial decarbonization strategies are a key way to spread out costs to make them more
acceptable to consumers.

A goal of 100% elimination of fossil fuels may be counterproductive. Many papers have
touched on the disproportionate results where the last 10% of efforts consume the same

122 of 332


--- page 123 ---
resources as the first 90%. Let's identify the biggest sources of ghg that are the easiest to
remedy first.

COSTS

Another challenge is costs to property owners. This report has spelled them out as best
possible given the uncertainty of future funds. The equity component for LMI housing presents
its own challenges: condos represent a challenge because of limited physical (23" door) utility
space, condo-hoa rules, noise from adjoining walls. (Gas water heaters are always under 50
dB, HPVVH are never.) Roof mount solutions require complicated coordination that would be
more costly than gas replacement.

EOF2030

The previous city council supported an EOF 2030. A leader of a local climate group called this
“mathematically impossible”. City council folk have said that this is the shortest path to recall.
The fact is that fewer than 1% of homes are electrified. How can we possibly electrify the
remaining 99% in 7 years?

Look no further than page 30 of the report which shows that commercial systems are probably
cost-prohibitive.

City of San Mateo
Building Inventory and Market Segmentation Memorandum (Task 1)

family homes and estimating costs is typically only possible on a project-by-project basis.
This is because for most larger buildings, replacing a gas system with a similarly sized
electric system is cost-prohibitive and therefore, often requires building re-engineering to
determine appliance replacement and sizing options. For this reason, a detailed cost
analysis for larger multifamily and commercial buildings was not developed and included
here, Smaller buildings with domestic-sized space and water heating end uses, such as
small to medium-sized retail or service buildings, nave similar electrification costs as seen
for single-family homes.

These sorts of political statements are fine, because they move the needle, but in terms of
concrete municipal planning we should have a realistic plan. The original intent of EOF2030,
which was underwritten by dozens of environmental organizations, allowed for flexibility. For
example, 50% reduction in natural gas delivery by 2030?

What are other municipalities doing? One would think that the first order of business is
estimating the entire GHG emissions portfolio and setting goals based on that estimate. Can

Rincon recommend a different goal that is both reasonable and measurable? A framework for
setting goals?

SUGGESTIONS FOR IMPROVEMENT

123 of 332


--- page 124 ---
Rincon has experience performing the same assessment in Sacto and Berkeley. As with those,
this deliverable for San Mateo should be targeted at city government level. More technical
project details would helpful.

Single point of contact. The city should consider a conceirge approach that will support property
owners in this difficult transition. Other approaches such as bulk-purchase of appliance installs
are being tried in some cities without great success. In this model the city selects the vendor,
who does bulk electrification at a fixed prices.

Please address BAAQMD '27/'30 rule changes is a gating factor for electrification because ROB
could be mandatory, What is the current burn-out rate in the city of San Mateo?

Please show the total estimated ghg building emmisions by commercial / residential. Total ghg
reduction, number of appliances, PGE accounting for local gas consumption, etc. County stats
are a good start but all of the metrics should relative to the city.

page 3 figure 1: what does catalyst upgrade mean?

Building Segmentatiaon Analysis. Please include ownership of SFH and MFH because
purchasing patterns differ: renters will not electrify on their own, and working with landlords is a
different proposition. Ownership is key.

page 7: energy equity. Please identify challenges in LMI housing such as ownership, smaller
panel feeds, smaller spaces for equiment, landlord apathy. Would title 24 insulation take
precidence over electrification?

page 7: can you include building permit data? We have looked at rebate data from BAYREN
and PCE and there is good information here that can establish a baseline (costs, number of
systems, panel upgrade). Perhaps as a change order and separate deliverable that works out
details like this?

page 8: converting county numbers to estimated numbers for the city of san mateo would add
clarity.
what are the total commercial ghg / residential ghng numbers for comparison ?

pg 18: prewiring is a strategy that removes delays in HPVUH installation. This is being explored
by SVCE. Service upgrade can take weeks or months. Pre-wiringallows skilled electricical
contractors to safely wire and do "non-plumbing" ahead of time, promotes home-owner buy-in,
and distributes the workloads as non-emergency service over a few months.

pg 22: per tom kabat "watt diet" and others. The term "building electrical upgrade" is an umbrella
term that includes a panel upgrade option. People are confused because panel upgrade
sounds costly and is infrequent. According to a recent TECH report, in most cases a new panel
was nof required. ( https://techcleanca.com/public-data/ ). This study provides estimates for

124 of 332


--- page 125 ---
panel upgrades for about 15,000 houses in california. Electrification often requires electrical
upgrades and typically the existing panel will suffice. For example, load sharing devices can be
used to alleviate the nead for a new service. Many upgrades require an electrician, can be
expensive, but do not constitute a panel swap..

We need more discovery on out of bounds and low income cases. Given that fewer than 1% of
the SFH in San Mateo County have HPWH and an even smaller percentage are fully electrified,
there should be plenty of wiggle room to allow exceptions as we focus on the low hanging fruit.
Start on easy upgrades such as tract homes with similar floorplans and power drops.

Evidently commercial is the bigger challenge. Perhaps the city should focus on residential for
the next leg of the journey.

From: Brian Stewart

Sent: Monday, September 11, 2023 9:06 PM
To: Sustainability & Infrastructure Commission
Subject: Building Electrification

City of San Mateo Sustainability & Infrastructure Commission

Thank you for releasing the draft study, in response to the City Council’s approved objective for FY 23-24."Establish
policies to decarbonize and electrify existing buildings and eliminate natural gas use by 2030.”

Building electrification is one of the most important responses to the climate crisis. The framework provided by the
report authors, Rincon Consultants, will provide a clear path for every San Mateo homeowner, landlord, and commercial
property owner to take the one most direct, immediate step to stop the march of the climate crisis.

Multiple studies show that electrification is one of the most effective methods for reducing or even eliminating carbon
emissions from buildings. As the electric grid in California gets cleaner and cleaner, emissions from high efficiency
electric appliances become smaller and smaller. This is not true for gas burning devices.

Building electrification also has significant positive community benefits such as increased comfort, better indoor air
quality, lower energy costs and reduced outdoor air pollution. Building electrification can be implemented in ways that

also promote equity and reduce energy costs for those most burdened.

As the Rincon Consultants report notes, “Whole home electrification could be less expensive for building owners in San
Mateo than replacing appliances with gas once rebates are included."

We urge you to adopt this framework and push for measures to electrify buildings in San Mateo as quickly and equitably
as possible. Action such as this is urgently needed in all our cities across the country.

Thank you,

Brian Stewart
Founder
Electrify Now

From: fert Whitenair

Sent: Tuesday, September 12, 2023 9:52 AM
To: Sustainability & Infrastructure Commission
Subject: Support for Decarbonization

Dear Sustainability and Infrastructure Commissioners:

| want to thank the Commission for its leadership and foresight in implementing the decarbonization objective
for the City of San Mateo and for releasing the draft study for review. San Mateo is one of just a handful of U.S.
cities taking decarbonization as seriously as the issue deserves.

Also, | urge you to continue the forward momentum on this process by supporting decarbonization in every
way possible, especially by focusing on building electrification.

Eliminating fossil fuel appliances from our city structures is urgent for the following reasons:

1) The warming climate, which is directly related to emissions of natural gas/methane, will have damaging
effects on the lives and livelihoods of San Mateans. We have seen that impacts include everything from
extreme heat (which is dangerous to children, the elderly, and the significant number of residents who lack air
conditioning), fiercer and wetter storms, and more frequent and more destructive wildfires. These are not crises
that San Mateans want, nor deserve. These are crises that interact and overlap since climate change is a
threat multiplier.

2) The methane gas we burn comes from dangerous practices such as hydraulic fracturing that pollute the
water and air and imperil frontline communities.

3) Burning methane in our homes here in San Mateo is an unsafe practice with many identified health risks, for
instance, from gas stoves.

4) Switching to electric will reduce costs in the long run (and far sooner when including incentives). Rewiring
America’s electrification guide estimates "Households will save on average $1,800 a year by going electric". As
technologies begin to scale, the costs will drop further. NREL reported in a recent model / study that "Higher
levels of electrification with rapid advancements in the cost and performance of end-use electric technologies

can result in net energy system savings—up to $800 billion in the high electrification scenario.”

5) Importantly, here in the Bay Area we are fortunate to have certain municipalities and CCAs in the vanguard
of electrification. For example, a wide variety of existing Peninsula Clean Energy rebates, newer tax credits
created by the Inflation Reduction Act, and other available funding makes electrification more affordable (as
the Rincon report indicates: "Whole home electrification could be less expensive for building owners in San
Mateo.

Thank you for your consideration of this important issue.
Teri Whitehair

Be The Change
Vegan Cooking with Purposel - YouTube

extreme heat (which is dangerous to children, the elderly, and the significant number of residents who lack air

the Rincon report indicates: "Whole home electrification could be less expensive for building owners in San
Mateo than replacing appliances with gas, once rebates are included").

Those who oppose decarbonization and want to choose business as usual are merely in denial that the future
is already getting hotter, more expensive, less predictable, and more deadly. | choose not to let that scenario
play out. Decarbonization is the better choice both from a moral and financial standpoint. With so many
common-sense reasons to accelerate electrification in San Mateo, | am hopeful that you will continue to
facilitate our progress on this issue.

Thank you for your dedicated service and commitment to the public welfare.

Subject: Electrification Mandate Upon Replacement of Gas Appliances

Dear Sustainability and Infrastructure Commission,

The public puts its trust in you. How can San Mateans trust a
commission like yours that suddenly fast-tracks a mandate to start
in 2025, only one and half years away, that you had originally
agreed to implement in 2030?

Who are you listening to? Certainly not to the majority of people of
San Mateo who have no agenda, who may choose gas over
electrical appliances upon upgrade (due to functionality, aesthetics,
or aging) for many reasons: The onerous cost of and extended wait
times involved with updating their electrical panel; fears of electrical
grid overload and power outages; fears of freezing in winter should
their electricity go out; being unable to cook during outages (unlike
electric, many gas ranges will still work without the electric pilot),
and more. All these situations simply magnify when a homeowner
suddenly has a gas appliance fail or break. That is an emergency
situation that will require IMMEDIATE action, and an IMMEDIATE
solution. You cannot ask people to wait weeks for a new furnace,
water heater, range/oven, or clothes dryer.

You claim to want to hear from all stakeholders. Let's see exactly

who you listen to. Lurge you to go back to your original
implementation date of 2030, and instead use this time to get
plans and infrastructure in place, to include:

139 of 332


--- page 140 ---
- Exempting replacements due to unplanned/emergency
appliance failure. Allow the replacement of sudden failed
gas appliances with gas appliances, and not
require replacement with electrical appliances. This will
give homeowners time to upgrade electrical panels.

- Give homeowners the option to switch to electric
appliances for both emergency and planned
replacement. Provide rebates and incentives to
encourage the switch.

- Ensure availability/accessibility to rebates/incentives for
ALL impacted homeowners, and additional rebates for
elderly/disadvantaged/low income.

- Limit the 2025 electrification mandate to NEW
construction and major remodels ONLY.

You are putting the cart before the horse in the race for a carbon-
neutral footprint, and in doing so, you SIGNIFICANTLY impact the
lives of 100,000 San Mateo residents. The horse may be ready,
but the cart and all its components are not. Take the time, and do it
right.

Thank you.

Lisa Vande Voorde
San Mateo Resident

--- page 142 ---
From: sue

Sent: Tuesday, September 12, 2023 5:20 PM
To: Sustainability & Infrastructure Commission
Subject: Building electrification

Dear Sustainability & Infrastructure Commissioners,

| am grateful to the Commission for its leadership and foresight in implementing the decarbonization objective
for the City of San Mateo and for releasing the draft study for review. | am proud that San Mateo is one of just
a handful of U.S. cities taking decarbonization as seriously as the issue deserves. At this time, however, |
would also like to urge you to continue the forward momentum on this process. We need to keep supporting
decarbonization —through all means possible, but specifically, by focusing on building electrification.

It is worth it to eliminate fossil fuel appliances from our city structures for the following reasons:

1) The warming climate, which is directly related to emissions of natural gas/methane, will have damaging
effects on the lives and livelihoods of residents of San Mateo. We have seen that impacts include everything
from extreme heat (which is dangerous to children, the elderly, and the significant number of residents who
lack air conditioning), fiercer and wetter storms, and more frequent and more destructive wildfires. These are
not crises that residents of San Mateo want, nor deserve. These are crises that interact and overlap since
climate change is a threat multiplier.

2) The methane gas we burn comes from dangerous practices such as hydraulic fracturing that pollute the
water and air and imperil frontline communities.

3) Burning methane in our homes here in San Mateo is an unsafe practice with many identified health risks, for
instance, from gas stoves.

4) Switching to electric will reduce costs in the long run (and far sooner when including incentives). Rewiring
America's electrification guide estimates "Households will save on average $1,800 a year by going electric’. As
technologies begin to scale, the costs will drop further. NREL reported in a recent model / study that "Higher
levels of electrification with rapid advancements in the cost and performance of end-use electric technologies

can result in net energy system savings—up to $800 billion in the high electrification scenario."

5) Importantly, here in the Bay Area we are fortunate to have certain municipalities and CCAs in the vanguard
of electrification. For example, a wide variety of existing Peninsula Clean Energy rebates, newer tax credits
created by the Inflation Reduction Act, and other available funding makes electrification more affordable (as
the Rincon report indicates: “Whole home electrification could be less expensive for building owners in San
Mateo than replacing appliances with gas, once rebates are included’).

Those who oppose decarbonization and want to choose business as usual are merely in denial that the future
is already getting hotter, more expensive, less predictable, and more deadly. | choose not to let that scenario
play out. Decarbonization is the better choice both from a moral and financial standpoint. With so many
common-sense reasons to accelerate electrification in San Mateo, | am hopeful that you will continue to
facilitate our progress on this issue,

Thank you for your dedicated service and commitment to the public welfare.

Sue Blockstein
San Mateo resident

framework provided by Rincon will provide a clear path for every San Mateo homeowner,

the march of the climate crisis: Electrify Everything!

From: a

Sent: Tuesday, September 12, 2023 7:41 PM

To: Sustainability & Infrastructure Commission

Subject: Support for the City of San Mateo Decarbonization Goals and Strategy

Dear Commissioners,

Thank you for your efforts to set and rigorously evaluate pathways toward San Mateo’s ambitious 2030 “End of Flow”
goal, Reaching this goal will rightly be a challenge and it demonstrates the level of commitment that the climate crisis
calls for.

San Mateo is playing an exemplary role by doing its detailed study of its situation and creating specific plans to
overcome the barriers to success. Peninsula communities share a strong and common interest in making it easier for
residents and businesses to play their part in going electric. The Rincon study already serves as a well informed and
transparent framework with specific criteria, building and device inventories and incremental retrofit cost data required
in order to rationally evaluate decarbonization strategies. The simple, common sense rule guiding the work, to electrify
on replacement wherever feasible, from now on is easily understandable. This is the quickest and cheapest route to
meaningful climate protection and the important co-benefits described in the report.

Please follow through in itemizing and prioritizing the many smart actions mentioned in the report. The market is
changing rapidly as new solutions and approaches are introduced and new incentives at the state and federal level
arrive. The Advance Water Heating Initiative field study reports that the new 110 volt heat pump water heaters are
satisfying the large majority of study customers and reducing energy use by 85%. There are an increasing number of low
current and circuit sharing devices reducing the need for panel upgrades. It will be useful for San Mateo to capture the
installation data in some detail, highlight success stories and share best practices that local building owners experience
in order to rapidly raise awareness and confidence in the electrification process. This kind of granular data will also help
the city improve and expand its programs.

San Mateo’s example is all the more important given the coming Air District emission standards. | am sure that other
cities and utilities in the whole Peninsula area will be working hard with your community to demonstrate the feasibility
of building electrification leading up to an Air District assessment in 2025.

Sincerely and with Friendly Regards,

Bret Andersen, Carbon Free Palo Alto

From: Rita Armstrong

Sent: Tuesday, September 12, 2023 8:34 PM
To: Sustainability & Infrastructure Commission; City Council (San Mateo)
Subject: Fast-track banning of gas appliances

C'mon. Are you really going to make residents of San Mateo convert existing gas
stoves and other appliances from gas to something else? Even cities which have
adopted a no-gas policy apply the ruling to new construction and remodels of existing
facilities only. Are you really going to make me take out my fully-functioning gas stove
and replace it with something else? Replacing gas appliances would be enormously
expensive, especially if the space in question is a kitchen. Replacement would involve
electrical work, cabinetry re-work, and probably flooring work as well. You're talking
about half a kitchen remodel for equipment that is currently functional. What a waste of
time and money. And how arrogant are you to think that this is no big deal? I'm
appalled.

To be clear, I fully support conversion to other energy sources in cases of new
construction or remodels, and would willingly participate with this type of ruling. What
you're proposing is costly and needless. The only people who would benefit are
contractors and appliance vendors. Are they making contributions to your campaign?

Why don't you focus on things that would make a tangible and appreciable difference for
San Mateans, like more and better bike lanes? better recycling efforts, especially in
public facilities like parks? closing off more downtown streets for foot and bike traffic?
replacing weak and dangerous trees with more sustainable varieties? Converting
dormant lots (like the one at the corner of El Camino and 3rd) into parklets until a better
use can be found? I could go on and on. Call me at the number below if you want more
ideas.

Rita Armstron

not to charge their cars to not stress the power grid.

residents before you decide whether to fast track the switchover to electric. It definitely should not be mandated to be
done all at once. As appliances need replacing is the common sense way to do it. The expense for residents is not just
paying for the appliance. Many houses need electrical work to support the increased demand.

From: Cheryl Weide

Sent: Tuesday, September 12, 2023 10:01 PM
To: Sustainability & Infrastructure Commission
Subject: Support elimination of Fossil Fuel appliances

| appreciate your leadership in the City of San Mateo's decarbonization efforts and the release of the draft study for
review. San Mateo's commitment to decarbonization is commendable.

| strongly support eliminating fossil fuel appliances from the city's structures, primarily for health and financial reasons:

* Local Health Impacts: Gas appliances pose identified health risks. The Bay Area Air Quality Management District
recently passed Rule 9-4 and 9-6 to eliminate future gas water heaters and HVAC systems due to the health
effects of nitrogen oxide and particulate matter. The staff report estimates doing so would save up to 85 lives
and 15,000 asthma attacks a year. Note, this is for outdoor air. Indoor air is likely worse.

e Regional/Global Health Impacts: Burning natural gas/methane contributes to a warming climate with adverse
effects on residents’ lives, from extreme heat to more destructive wildfires. Extreme heat is the leading weather
cause of death. Climate change multiplies these threats.

¢ Environmental Concerns: Methane gas extraction methods like hydraulic fracturing harm our environment and
frontline communitie

* Cost Savings: Transitioning to electric will bring long-term cost savings, benefiting households by an estimated
$1,800 annually. As technologies advance, costs will further decrease.

* Local Support: San Mateo benefits from local initiatives and incentives, making electrification affordable and
accessible

* Simplified Electrification: The grid is becoming more reliable, and new electric products reduce the need for
extensive rewiring. Streamlined processes and local workforce development support electrification.

Denying the urgency of decarbonization ignores the reality of an unhealthy, hotter, costlier, and less predictable future.
Decarbonization aligns with both moral and financial considerations. With numerous compelling reasons to accelerate
electrification in San Mateo, | urge you to continue facilitating our progress.

Thank you for your dedicated service and commitment to public welfare.

Sincerely,

Cheryl Weiden

report estimates doing so would save up to 85 lives and 15,000 asthma attacks a year. Note,

this is for outdoor air. Indoor air is likely worse.

Regional/Global Health Impacts: Burning natural gas/methane contributes to a warming
climate with adverse effects on residents’ lives, from extreme heat to more destructive
wildfires. Extreme heat is the leading weather cause of death. Climate change multiplies these

threats.

Environmental Concerns: Methane gas extraction methods like hydraulic fracturing harm our

environment and frontline communities.

Cost Savings: Transitioning to electric will bring long-term cost savings, benefiting households

by an estimated $1,800 annually. As technologies advance, costs will further decrease.

Local Support: San Mateo benefits from local initiatives and incentives, making electrification

affordable and accessible.

157 of 332


--- page 158 ---
Simplified Electrification: The grid is becoming more reliable, and new electric products reduce
the need for extensive rewiring. Streamlined processes and local workforce development

support electrification.

Denying the urgency of decarbonization ignores the reality of an unhealthy, hotter, costlier,
and less predictable future. Decarbonization aligns with both moral and financial
considerations. With numerous compelling reasons to accelerate electrification in San Mateo,

{ urge you to continue facilitating our progress.
Thank you for your dedicated service and commitment to public welfare.

Dear Sustainability and Infrastructure Commissioners,

As a youth, climate change has been a major source of anxiety in my life. | have missed several days of school because of
wildfires largely caused by climate change. My neighborhood in San Mateo is only 7 feet above sea level. | have heard
predictions that by 2030, my home may be impacted by severe flooding. | am afraid that, without direct action, these
impacts will only worsen.

Because of this, | would like to thank you for all of the work you have done regarding sustainability in San Mateo. The
actions you have taken to address climate change, such as implementing reach codes to bring about more sustainable
buildings, have been a source of hope for me and many other youth. | believe that direct action, such as that which you
have taken, is critical to taking action against climate change.

As such, | think it is vital to continue taking steps to electrify San Mateo. Electrification is one of the best ways to reduce
carbon emissions and help address climate change. Along with this, research suggests that electric technologies will help
to save citizens hundreds of dollars, while also replacing much more dangerous appliances such as gas stoves.

For the future of San Mateo citizens, and especially the youth, | urge you to continue fighting for electrification and
decarbonization.

Sincerely,

Konstantinos Paparrizos

money. The future is clear. With the Air Resource Board's goal of prohibiting the sale of new
gas furnaces and water heaters by 2030, we will all need to transition away from fossil fuels

soon. Why not help pave the way for other cities to follow your lead?

| can sense the change in people's attitudes. While lunching with an elderly friend today who

her gas stove taken away. But a big surprise: Today she said she was looking forward to
replacing her dirty old furnace with help from local, state and federal rebates because it would
her asthma. An added bonus she didn't even know about: her heat pump space heater will

also provide air conditioning,

--- page 180 ---
From: Yutian Cheng a
Sent: Wednesday, September 13, 2023 8:23 AM

Cc: Sustainability & Infrastructure Commission
Subject: Reach code for decarbonization

Dear Sustainability & Infrastructure Commissioners,

As a resident in San Mateo County, | am grateful to the Commission for its leadership and
foresight in implementing the decarbonization objective for the City of San Mateo and for
releasing the draft study for review. San Mateo is one of just a handful of U.S. cities taking
decarbonization as seriously as the issue deserves. Kudos to you. At this time, however, | would
also like to urge you to continue the forward momentum on this process. We need to keep
supporting decarbonization —through all means possible, but specifically, by focusing on building
electrification.

It is worth it to eliminate fossil fuel appliances from our city structures for the following reasons:
1) The warming climate, which is directly related to emissions of natural gas/methane, will have
damaging effects on the lives and livelihoods of San Mateans. We have seen that impacts include
everything from extreme heat (which is dangerous to children, the elderly, and the significant
number of residents who lack air conditioning), fiercer and wetter storms, and more frequent and
more destructive wildfires. These are not crises that San Mateans want, nor deserve. These are
crises that interact and overlap since climate change is a threat multiplier,

2) The methane gas we burn comes from dangerous practices such as hydraulic fracturing that
pollute the water and air and imperil frontline communities.

3) Burning methane in our homes here in San Mateo is an unsafe practice with many identified
health risks, for instance, from gas stoves.

4) Switching to electric will reduce costs in the long run (and far sooner when including
incentives), Rewiring America's electrification guide estimates "Households will save on average
$1,800 a year by going electric". As technologies begin to scale, the costs will drop further, NREL
reported in a recent model / study that "Higher levels of electrification with rapid advancements
in the cost and performance of end-use electric technologies can result in net energy system
savings—up to $800 billion in the high electrification scenario."

5) Importantly, here in the Bay Area we are fortunate to have certain municipalities and CCAs in
the vanguard of electrification. For example, a wide variety of existing Peninsula Clean Energy
rebates, newer tax credits created by the Inflation Reduction Act, and other available funding
makes electrification more affordable (as the Rincon report indicates: “Whole home electrification
could be less expensive for building owners in San Mateo than replacing appliances with gas, once
rebates are included"),
Those who oppose decarbonization and want to choose business as usual are merely in denial
that the future is already getting hotter, more expensive, less predictable, and more deadly. |

choose not to let that scenario play out. Decarbonization is the better choice both from a moral
1

180 of 332


--- page 181 ---
and financial standpoint. With so many common-sense reasons to accelerate electrification in San
Mateo, | am hopeful that you will continue to facilitate our progress on this issue.

Thank you for your dedicated service and commitment to the public welfare.

Sincerely,
Yutian Cheng

of the draft study for review. San Mateo's commitment to decarbonization is commendable.

| strongly support eliminating fossil fuel appliances from the city's structures, primarily for

health and financial reasons:

Local Health Impacts: Gas appliances pose identified health risks. The Bay Area Air Quality
Management District recently passed Rule 9-4 and 9-6 to eliminate future gas water heaters
and HVAC systems due to the health effects of nitrogen oxide and particulate matter. The staff
report estimates doing so would save up to 85 lives and 15,000 asthma attacks a year. Note,

this is for outdoor air. Indoor air is likely worse.

Regional/Global Health Impacts: Burning natural gas/methane contributes to a warming
climate with adverse effects on residents’ lives, from extreme heat to more destructive
wildfires. Extreme heat is the leading weather cause of death. Climate change multiplies these

threats.

Environmental Concerns: Methane gas extraction methods like hydraulic fracturing harm our

environment and frontline communities.

Cost Savings: Transitioning to electric will bring long-term cost savings, benefiting households

by an estimated $1,800 annually. As technologies advance, costs will further decrease.

Local Support: San Mateo benefits from local initiatives and incentives, making electrification

affordable and accessible.

Simplified Electrification: The grid is becoming more reliable, and new electric products reduce
the need for extensive rewiring. Streamlined processes and local workforce development

support electrification.

and less predictable future. Decarbonization aligns with both moral and financial

Frum, Jdlits FIYUIC es

To: Sustainability & Infrastructure Commission
Subject: decarbonize San Mateo

Dear Sustainability and Infrastructure Commissioners,

Thank you for beginning to get the ball rolling on decarbonization in San Mateo. As the many fires, floods, and deadly
heat waves this past year have shown, the time when any person of conscience could treat this as a back-burner issue is
long gone. Fortunately, energy technology has improved so greatly in the past decade that we don't have to make
sacrifices to eliminate most of our climate pollution. Electrification of buildings is a win-win-win: it makes our air cleaner
right now, it saves us money after a couple years (and from then on out), and it lets us cut out a huge part of our climate
emissions. It's such an obviously good thing for our community and for the future that we should be going full speed to
do it even if there weren't massive federal subsidies to make it even more beneficial to San Mateo residents.

| hope you will also consider measures that put renters who wish to decarbonize their buildings and transport in a
stronger position, so that clean air is not a luxury for the affluent.

Change is coming, whether we like it or not. In one direction, there are fires, heat waves, and floods. In the other,
there's a clean, prosperous, technologically advanced world. | think it's clear which path we should take,

--James Higbie, San Mateo Resident

Dear Sustainability & Infrastructure Commissioners,

| appreciate your leadership in the City of San Mateo's decarbonization efforts and the release of the draft study for
review. San Mateo's commitment to decarbonization is commendable.

| strongly support eliminating fossil fuel appliances from the city's structures, primarily for health and financial reasons
as outlined below:

Local Health Impacts: Gas appliances pose identified health risks. The Bay Area Air Quality Management District recently
passed Rule 9-4 and 9-6 to eliminate future gas water heaters and HVAC systems due to the health effects of nitrogen
oxide and particulate matter. The staff report estimates doing so would save up to 85 lives and 15,000 asthma attacks a
year. Note, this is for outdoor air. Indoor air is likely worse.

Regional/Global Health tmpacts: Burning natural gas/methane contributes to a warming climate with adverse effects on
residents' lives, from extreme heat to more destructive wildfires. Extreme heat is the leading weather cause of

death. Climate change multiplies these threats.

Environmental Concerns: Methane gas extraction methods like hydraulic fracturing harm our environment and frontline
communities.

Cost Savings: Transitioning to electric will bring long-term cost savings, benefiting households by an estimated $1,800
annually. As technologies advance, costs will further decrease.

Local Support: San Mateo benefits from local initiatives and incentives, making electrification affordable and accessible.
Simplified Electrification: The grid is becoming more reliable every day, and new electric products reduce the need for
extensive rewiring. Peninsula Clean Energy is streamlining processes and developing a one-stop shop to assist
homeowners with electrification (whole-home and partial). Community groups are working with unions and contractors
to develop the electrification workforce.

Denying the urgency of decarbonization ignores the reality of an unhealthy, hotter, costlier, and less predictable future.
Decarbonization aligns with both moral and financial considerations. With numerous compelling reasons to accelerate
electrification in San Mateo, | urge you to continue facilitating our progress.

Thank you for your dedicated service and commitment to public welfare.

--- page 214 ---
Sent: Wednesday, September 13, 2023 10:20 AM
To: Sustainability & Infrastructure Commission
Subject: Support decarbonization through building electrification

Dear Sustainability & Infrastructure Commissioners,

Thank you for your leadership in implementing the decarbonization objective for the City of San Mateo and for
releasing the draft study for review. San Mateo is one of just a handful of U.S. cities taking decarbonization as
seriously as the issue deserves. Kudos to you. At this time, | would like to urge you to continue the forward
momentum on this process. We need to keep supporting decarbonization —through all means possible, but
specifically, by focusing on building electrification.

| support eliminating fossil fuel appliances from the City’s structures for the following reasons:

1) The warming climate, which is directly related to emissions of natural gas/methane, will have damaging
effects on the lives and livelihoods of community members. We have seen that impacts include everything from
extreme heat (which is dangerous to children, the elderly, and the significant number of residents who lack air
conditioning), fiercer and wetter storms, and more frequent and more destructive wildfires. These are crises
that interact and overlap since climate change is a threat multiplier.

2) The methane gas we burn comes from dangerous practices such as hydraulic fracturing that pollute the
water and air and imperil frontline communities.

3) Burning methane in our homes here in San Mateo is an unsafe practice with many identified health risks, for
instance, from gas stoves.

4) Switching to electric will reduce costs in the long run (and far sooner when including incentives). Rewiring
America’s electrification guide estimates "Households will save on average $1,800 a year by going electric’. As
technologies begin to scale, the costs will drop further. NREL reported in a recent model / study that "Higher
levels of electrification with rapid advancements in the cost and performance of end-use electric technologies

can result in net energy system savings—up to $800 billion in the high electrification scenario."

5) Importantly, here in the Bay Area we are fortunate to have certain municipalities and CCAs in the vanguard
of electrification. For example, a wide variety of existing Peninsula Clean Energy rebates, newer tax credits
created by the Inflation Reduction Act, and other available funding makes electrification more affordable (as
the Rincon report indicates: “Whole home electrification could be less expensive for building owners in San
Mateo than replacing appliances with gas, once rebates are included").

6) Electrification is getting easier for residents, and will continue to do so. The grid is getting more reliable all
the time. New electric appliances/products have been coming onto the market that will strain the grid less, and
significantly minimize the necessity for rewiring work and service upgrades. PCE, the IRA, and the state are
creating streamline permit processes. PCE is developing a one-stop shop to assist homeowners with their
electrification retrofits. Local organizations are working with loca! unions and contractors to develop the
electrification workforce. This is no longer the same era that it was for the early “home electrifiers.”

Those who oppose decarbonization and want to choose business as usual are merely in denial that the future
is already getting hotter, more expensive, less predictable, and more deadly. Decarbonization is the better
choice both from a moral and financial standpoint. With so many common-sense reasons to accelerate
electrification in San Mateo, | am hopeful that you will continue to facilitate our progress on this issue.

1

214 of 332


--- page 215 ---
Thank you for your dedicated service and commitment to the public welfare.
Sincerely,

Mark Hoffberg
Retired physicist

Subject: SIC comments - Decarbonization, BPAC & Climate Action, and #WeekWithoutDriving
Dear SIC,

City Staff have done an amazing job preparing these reports and have been working hard!
Please see my comments and questions ahead of the upcoming meeting:

- Support Decarbonizing Existing Buildings
- Ped/Bike Update & Climate Action Plan
- #WeekWithoutDriving

Support Decarbonizing Existing Buildings

Both | and my mother have experienced gas leaks at places we have rented in the city where we've had to call the
company to come out. And having a gas stove caused major health concerns for my mom to the point where she had to
move. Eliminating gas from buildings benefits human health and the environment.

Ped/Bike Update & Climate Action Plan

In the city’s CAP, the reductions to come from ped/bike and transit are very small. Most reductions related to
transportation are expected to come from TDM/TOD and clean fleet/EV. But even with all the reductions laid out in the
plan, the transportation sector will still be the highest GHG emissions in San Mateo. Does the city know what needs to
happen to bring the GHG emissions from transportation to zero? Why do ped/bike investments bring so little in GHG
reductions for the CAP?

https://www.cityofsanmateo.ore/2769/Climate-Action-Plan

#WeekWithoutDriving
This week San Mateo County is encouraging decision makers to try a #WeekWithoutDriving.

The goal of the #WeekWithoutDriving is for those who have the option to drive to understand the barriers and
challenges that non-drivers face, with the goal to create better communities for all — to experience it yourself — not
just walking, rolling or taking transit to eliminate a car trip, but for everything you do.

The Week Without Driving campaign is national this year (10/2-8), but San Mateo County’s week (9/10-16) is different
than the national dates due to the school district’s fall break.

https://americawalks.org/week-without-drivin

https://americawalks.org/campaigns/national-week-without-drivin
